        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/cssc.201701484')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d9a08e95d5594e6bbd372e3ccd37365e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c4ad18b9-49f6-4948-860c-48b0f22828af'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d50642054" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Flexible and efficient cathode</b>: Flower-like cuprous tetracyanoquinodimethane (CuTCNQ) nanorods in situ anchored on a flexible carbon matrix are prepared by combining the routes of electrospinning and solution reaction. As flexible sodium-ion battery cathode, they exhibit a high specific capacity of 252 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, high average voltage of 3.2 V and long-term cyclability of 1200 cycles.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c77cfcb1-08f2-445e-9753-c5bcb73e08c8/cssc201701484-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c77cfcb1-08f2-445e-9753-c5bcb73e08c8/cssc201701484-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/214cc899-86ee-485e-a39c-4bc4fb6468a1/cssc201701484-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c77cfcb1-08f2-445e-9753-c5bcb73e08c8/cssc201701484-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d50642057" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Metal–organic compounds are a family of electrode materials with structural diversity and excellent thermal stability for rechargeable batteries. Here, we fabricated a hierarchical nanocomposite with metal–organic cuprous tetracyanoquinodimethane (CuTCNQ) in a 3 D conductive carbon nanofibers (CNFs) network by in situ growth, and evaluated it as flexible cathode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). CuTCNQ in such flexible composite electrode is able to exhibit a high capacity of 252 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and highly reversible stability for 1200 cycles within the voltage range of 2.5–4.1 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). A high specific energy of 762 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> was obtained with high average potential of 3.2 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The in situ-formed electroactive metal–organic composites with tailored nanoarchitecture provide a promising alternative choice for high-performance cathode materials in SIBs with high energy.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc201701484-sec-0001">
         
         <p>Recently, room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received tremendous attention for electrochemical energy storage applications owing to their low cost and the abundance of sodium compared with lithium.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0001_R_d50642046e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></span> For SIBs, great progress has been achieved on anode materials, but cathode materials face a big challenge owing to their limited capacity and some other causes for unsatisfactory performances.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0002_R_d50642046e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></span> Researchers have mainly focused on inorganic cathode materials such as Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0003_R_d50642046e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Energy">3</a></span> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>,<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0004_R_d50642046e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></span> and Prussian blue analogues,<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0005_R_d50642046e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Commun.">5</a></span> but most of these materials are currently plagued by insufficient cycling or low specific capacity owing to limited redox sites. Recently, metal–organic compounds,<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0006_R_d50642046e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></span> which are composed of metal ions coordinated to organic ligands, are emerging as state-of-the-art electrode materials and have drawn more and more interest. Férey et al. and Zhang et al. reported that multiple valent transition metal ions and organic ligands are able to show electrochemical activity in metal–organic compounds.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0007_R_d50642046e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></span> Metal–organic compounds can be designed to consist of electroactive metal ions and organic ligands ions, so that multielectron transfer redox reactions can be achieved to enhance the Na-storage capacity. Moreover, the transition metal ions participate in the electrochemical reaction at high potentials, which is helpful to raise the battery voltage. In our previous work,<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0008_R_d50642046e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">8</a></span> we demonstrated a metal–organic compound of CuTCNQ as SIB cathode material showing a very high specific capacity. We found that the cationic Cu<sup>+</sup> and anionic TCNQ<sup>−</sup> could simultaneously participate in the redox reaction with a three-electron transfer process. Benefiting from the strong inductive effect of multiple cyano groups, the average potential was as high as 3.1 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and the theoretical specific energy density was more than 900 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, showing promise for practical applications. However, as CuTCNQ is an organic-based compound, one may concern is its structural stability and electronic conductivity. In addition, if we cannot prepare CuTCNQ in a small size or in a state of composite with other materials, the performance cannot be improved. Therefore, it is desirable to fabricate the CuTCNQ nanocomposites to improve the electronic conductivity and hence the electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>As we know, electrospinning is a versatile technology to prepare 3 D conductive networks and an efficient way to prepare nanosized materials.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0009_R_d50642046e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></span> Herein, we employed a facile in situ synthetic method by combining the routes of electrospinning and solution reaction to attain a 3 D nanostructured CuTCNQ/CNFs (CNFs: carbon nanofibers) composite with flower-like CuTCNQ nanorods anchored on 1 D CNFs. The obtained CuTCNQ/CNFs composite was directly used as a binder-free electrode for SIB, in which CuTCNQ exhibits a high specific capacity of 252 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C. More importantly, the flexible electrode provides a more stable long-term cyclability for 1200 cycles, improved rate performance (89 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C), and higher specific energy of 762 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> than pristine CuTCNQ for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The synthesis process of CuTCNQ/CNFs is illustrated in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0001">1</a>. First, Cu nanoparticles were anchored onto a flexible CNFs network to form Cu/CNFs composite by electrospinning and, subsequently, thermal treatment in reducing atmosphere was done. During the thermal treatment, all Cu(OAc)<sub>2</sub> was reduced to elemental Cu; the powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern is shown in Figure S1 (Supporting Information). After the Cu/CNFs were soaked in the TCNQ solution, CuTCNQ nanorods were grown in situ and assembled into a flower-like structure on a flexible carbon matrix. The whole process effectively avoids the direct thermal treatment of CuTCNQ at high temperature and prevents CuTCNQ decomposition (Figure S2) during the carbonization process. In Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0002">2</a> a, digital photographs of the CuTCNQ/CNFs composite after soaking show a black flexible film. The thin film is pliable and can be bent in different directions without cracking. After being completely dry, the flexible freestanding CuTCNQ/CNFs composite was directly used as a binder-free SIB electrode. In Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0002">2</a> b, XRD patterns of both CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ (CuTCNQ/PDs) demonstrate a well-defined crystal structure of CuTCNQ phase I, in which five main diffraction peaks are located at 10.89°, 15.48°, 17.36°, 22.07°, and 22.37°, corresponding to the (011), (002), (021), (022), and (013) planes.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0010_R_d50642046e478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></span> For CuTCNQ/CNFs, an extra diffraction peak appears at 26.33°, corresponding to the (002) plane of graphitic carbon produced during the carbonization process. The morphology of the formed Cu/C fibers was investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0002">2</a> c, the carbon fibers formed a 3 D crossed network nanostructure with an approximate average diameter of 500 nm. The Cu particles are uniformly inlaid in the carbon fibers. Clear lattice fringes of Cu crystal are found in the high-resolution TEM (see the inset of Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0002">2</a> d). Subsequently, CuTCNQ/CNFs was obtained by soaking the Cu/C nanofibers in TCNQ acetonitrile solution for a period of time. Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0002">2</a> e shows the SEM image of the CuTCNQ/CNFs. Interestingly, the CuTCNQ nanorods have an average length of 300–400 nm and diameter of about 100 nm, and a flower-like morphology is attained, which blossomed from the Cu nanoparticles. The CuTCNQ nanoflowers almost completely cover the carbon fibers, which can provide a continuous conductive network between the flowers. The energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) mappings (Figure1 S3) of CuTCNQ/CNFs reveal a uniform distribution of Cu and N within the thin fibrous carbon network, and no impurities were detected. In comparison, the morphology of CuTCNQ/PDs in Figure S4 shows that an average particle size of CuTCNQ/PDs is about 10 μm. From the morphology comparison, we can clearly find that the growth of CuTCNQ is restricted by the fibrous carbon networks.
</p>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201701484-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a652dce0-3ea4-44d5-9b18-60b37eb1d3c2/cssc201701484-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a652dce0-3ea4-44d5-9b18-60b37eb1d3c2/cssc201701484-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/61a176cf-a143-4b00-83c5-c43e3bb716b2/cssc201701484-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a652dce0-3ea4-44d5-9b18-60b37eb1d3c2/cssc201701484-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201701484-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201701484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Digital photographs of flexible CuTCNQ/CNFs. b) XRD patterns of CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ/PDs. c) SEM image of Cu/C nanofibers. d) TEM image of Cu/C nanofiber, the insert is the high-magnification TEM image of Cu nanoparticle. e) Low-magnification SEM image of CuTCNQ/CNFs, demonstrating a hierarchical nanoarchitecture. f) High-magnification SEM image of CuTCNQ/CNFs, revealing the structural detail of an individual CuTCNQ nanoflower.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To investigate the electrochemical performance of CuTCNQ/CNFs as a binder-free cathode for SIBs, galvanostatic charge/discharge, cyclic voltammetry (CV), rate capability, and cycling tests were performed. The loadings of the active materials in the self-supported working electrodes were calculated based on the mass change before and after being soaked in the TCNQ acetonitrile solution, which are listed in Table S1. For comparison, CuTCNQ/PDs electrodes were also tested under the same conditions.</p>
         
         <p>The galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (0.1 C) are depicted in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0003">3</a> a. From open-circuit voltage (OCV) to 4.1 V, there are two flat plateaus at 3.6 and 4.0 V, achieving a charge capacity of 224 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, calculated based on the mass of CuTCNQ. The capacity of the carbon matrix at this potential range is only 1.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which can be neglected (Figure S5). These two smooth plateaus correspond to the oxidation reaction of TCNQ<sup>−</sup> and Cu<sup>+</sup>, respectively. During the discharge process, three distinct flat voltage plateaus appear at 3.8, 3.3, and 2.3 V. The plateau at 3.8 V is related to the reduction from Cu<sup>2+</sup> to Cu<sup>+</sup> and the plateau at 3.3 V is attributed to the transformation from TCNQ<sup>0</sup> to TCNQ<sup>−</sup>. Another plateau at 2.2 V is generated from further reduction of TCNQ<sup>−</sup> to TCNQ<sup>2−</sup>. The total discharge capacity is as high as 252 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to 84 % of the theoretical capacity of three-electron transfer reaction (300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). In the second cycle, three small voltage steps occur within the range of 2.4–3.3 V during the charge process, which are split from the oxidation reaction of TCNQ<sup>2−</sup> to TCNQ<sup>−</sup>. The second discharge profile coincides well with the first cycle, indicative of a highly reversible electrochemical reaction.</p>
         
         <p>To further identify the redox process of the CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ/PDs, CV measurements of the initial three cycles were performed within the voltage range of 2.0–4.1 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/ Na) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0003">3</a> b, three highly reversible reduction peaks appear at 2.1, 3.2, and 3.75 V, consistent with the charge/discharge curves. The oxidation process of TCNQ<sup>2−</sup> to TCNQ is also split into four stages in the range of 2.4–3.4 V, indicating that the dissociation process is divided into four steps. The oxidation peaks of TCNQ<sup>−</sup> to TCNQ<sup>0</sup> and Cu<sup>+</sup> to Cu<sup>2+</sup> are located at 3.6 and 4.0 V respectively. The highly overlapped curves of the 1st to 3rd cycles reveal the excellent reversibility of the redox reactions. As a comparison, the CV curves of CuTCNQ/PDs are represented in Figure S6. The redox peaks are shifted and do no coincide well, implying larger polarization and worse electrochemical reversibility. The charge/discharge performances of CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ/PDs at various current rates of 30 (0.1 C), 60 (0.2 C), 150 (0.5 C), 300 (1 C), and 600 (2 C) mA g<sup>−1</sup> were also investigated, as compared in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0003">3</a> c. The corresponding capacities were 252, 238, 207, 137, and 89 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for CuTCNQ/CNFs; and 239, 147, 114, 57, and 17 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for CuTCNQ/PDs. The capacities of the former are much higher than the latter, indicative of an excellent rate capability for CuTCNQ/CNFs. Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0003">3</a> d shows the cycling performance of CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ/PDs electrodes in the voltage range of 2.0–4.1 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 300 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. After 300 cycles, the capacity retention of the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode was maintained at 85 % with a capacity of 137 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, whereas the capacity of the CuTCNQ/PDs electrode decays rapidly to only 25 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. We narrowed the voltage range to 2.5–4.1 V to further investigate the cycling stability at 300 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0003">3</a> e, the electrode exhibits a capacity retention as high as 78 % even after extended 1200 cycles with a Coulombic efficiency near 100 %, demonstrating a superior long-term cycle stability. The outstanding cycling performance of the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode is profited from the synergistic effect of the nanostructure of CuTCNQ and the integration in highly conductive, intercrossed CNFs. The nanosized CuTCNQ flowers can significantly reduce the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path to ensure Na<sup>+</sup> passing through rapidly, and can enable more contact between the Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the active materials. Moreover, the uniform distribution of CuTCNQ nanorods in the fibrous carbon matrix is able to generate a balanced stress over the whole composite and maintain the integrity of the electrode upon prolonged cycling. The 3 D conductive network interlinked by 1 D carbon fibers can serve as a 3 D current collector to provide continuous channels for electron transport and hence improve the utilization of the active materials. Furthermore, the increased surface area (Figure S7) of CuTCNQ/CNFs can create more electrode/electrolyte interface, favoring the Na<sup>+</sup> ions transfer. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) curves for the CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ/PDs electrodes are compared in Figure S8. The Nyquist plots consist of a compressed semicircle in the high-medium frequency region corresponding to the charge-transfer resistance, and an oblique line in the low-frequency region represents Warburg impedance, which is associated with Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion into the electrode. A semicircle with a much smaller diameter and a steeper line is observed for the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode, suggesting a much lower charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion impedance in the 3 D network.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201701484-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0c38eabf-33ed-49e3-9f26-363d2f60190f/cssc201701484-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0c38eabf-33ed-49e3-9f26-363d2f60190f/cssc201701484-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b929c859-2288-4afc-9ecf-7824a7699efb/cssc201701484-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0c38eabf-33ed-49e3-9f26-363d2f60190f/cssc201701484-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The high specific capacity and high operating voltage are remarkable improvements for CuTCNQ/CNFs. A specific energy density as high as 762 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> was obtained. The specific energies versus cell voltages at different current densities are illustrated in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0004">4</a> a. Based on the average voltage of 3.0 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), the theoretical energy density of the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode can reach 900 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, assuming that the three electrons from the cationic redox couple of Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>+</sup> and anionic redox couples of TCNQ<sup>2−</sup>/TCNQ<sup>−</sup> and TCNQ<sup>−</sup>/TCNQ<sup>0</sup> are fully utilized (300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The Ragone plots in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0004">4</a> b summarize the trade-off between the specific power and power density of the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode in comparison with the performance of various reported advanced cathodes for SIBs. According to this plot, the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode presents much higher energy density throughout a wide range of charge/discharge rates.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201701484-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/838ffa17-0ec2-4257-b473-fa9a266098f6/cssc201701484-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/838ffa17-0ec2-4257-b473-fa9a266098f6/cssc201701484-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/206a2cdd-b725-4d77-adb6-fca854d12dc3/cssc201701484-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/838ffa17-0ec2-4257-b473-fa9a266098f6/cssc201701484-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201701484-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201701484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Specific energy densities of CuTCNQ/CNFs at different currents (PTCDA,<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0011" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0011_R_d50642046e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></span> Na4C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>,<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0012" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0012_R_d50642046e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Acta">13</a></span> Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>,<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0003" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0003_R_d50642046e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Energy">3</a></span> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>)<sup>[4]</sup>. b) Ragone plots of various advanced cathode materials.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement was performed to analyze the detailed information on the redox species in CuTCNQ/CNFs during the discharge process. High-resolution scans of Cu 2p are depicted in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0005">5</a> a. In the discharge stage at 3.8 V, the spin-orbit-coupled Cu 2p region fits well with a set of single peaks at 933.1 eV (2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 952.9 eV (2p<sub>1/2</sub>), indicating the existence of Cu<sup>+</sup>. Another predominant couple of peaks at 935.3 and 955.2 eV accompanied with the satellite peak at 942.2 eV confirm the presence of Cu<sup>2+</sup>. The coexistence of Cu<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> demonstrates an incomplete reduction from Cu<sup>2+</sup> to Cu<sup>+</sup>. When further charged to 3.7 V, the Cu<sup>2+</sup> species diminishes as the shake-up satellite peak disappears, and the couple peaks at 935.3 and 955.2 eV also vanish. There are only a set of Cu<sup>+</sup> signals left, suggesting that the Cu<sup>2+</sup> species have been fully transformed to Cu<sup>+</sup>. In the subsequent discharging process to 3.0 and 2.0 V, the valence states of Cu are in the form of Cu<sup>+</sup>. The couple of peaks slightly shift to the lower binding energy at 2.0 V, implying that Na<sup>+</sup> ions are intercalated into the CuTCNQ compounds. The unchanged charge state of Cu confirms that the discharge voltage plateaus at 3.3 and 2.3 V are correlated to the redox reaction of TCNQ anions. The N 1s peak can be used to distinguish the charged state of TCNQ.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0014_R_d50642046e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">14</a></span> Consequently, we tracked the high-resolution spectra of N 1s when discharging from 3.7 to 2.0 V, as shown in Figure <a href="#cssc201701484-fig-0005">5</a> b. At 3.7 V, a fitting peak of N 1s is located at 399.3 eV accompanied by a shake-up peak at 401.5 eV, which is characteristic for TCNQ<sup>0</sup>.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0014_R_d50642046e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">14</a></span> After discharging to 3.0 V, the fitted N 1s peak shifts to 398.6 eV with the satellite peak at 400.1 eV, which is in well agreement with the TCNQ<sup>−</sup> in CuTCNQ.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0015_R_d50642046e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></span> When discharged to 2.0 V, the N 1s peak and its shake-up signal further move back to 398.7 and 400.7 eV, respectively, providing evidence of the formation of TCNQ<sup>2−</sup>.<span><a href="#cssc201701484-bib-0015a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201701484-bib-0015a_R_d50642046e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15a - Chem. Mater.">15a</a></span> The above evolution of Cu cations and TCNQ<sup><i>x</i>−</sup> species demonstrates that both Cu cations and TCNQ anions are involved in the redox reactions during the charge/discharge process, resulting in a multielectron-transfer reaction with a high theoretical specific capacity of 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="cssc201701484-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eed221c1-86a6-44ad-ba4b-34f623fe50eb/cssc201701484-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/eed221c1-86a6-44ad-ba4b-34f623fe50eb/cssc201701484-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9a6ee244-5678-46f6-971e-71969d444c7a/cssc201701484-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eed221c1-86a6-44ad-ba4b-34f623fe50eb/cssc201701484-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201701484-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201701484" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>High-resolution XPS spectra of a) Cu 2p and b) N 1s for CuTCNQ/CNFs at different discharge state.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In summary, a novel metal–organic compound grown in situ on a flexible carbon nanofiber network (CuTCNQ/CNFs, TCNQ: cuprous tetracyanoquinodimethane) was fabricated and utilized as a freestanding cathode for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Benefited from the multielectron-transfer redox reactions and highly interconnected carbon fiber network, such metal–organic/carbon composite electrode exhibits a high specific capacity of 252 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and excellent rate capability with a capacity of 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C. Significantly, the CuTCNQ/CNFs electrode shows a long cycle life as demonstrated by over 1200 cycles in the voltage range of 2.5–4.0 V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), representing a highly reversible and stable electrochemical reaction. A high specific energy of 762 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> was obtained from the multi-redox reactions involving both Cu cations and TCNQ anions, which were further identified by ex situ XPS of Cu 2p and N 1s spectra. We believe that this work not only proves that the metal–organic compounds can serve as stable high-energy electrodes for SIBs, but also provides a facile method to fabricate the advanced composite of electroactive metal–organic compounds and conductive carbon network with enhanced electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201701484-sec-0002">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201701484-sec-0002-title"> Experimental Section</h3>
            
            <p><b>Synthesis of CuTCNQ/CNFs and CuTCNQ powder</b>: In a typical procedure, polyacrylonitrile (PAN, 1 g) and copper acetate (Cu(OAc)2, 0.5 g) were added in <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (10 mL) to prepare a precursor solution, and then the solution was used to prepare freestanding nanofibers by electrospinning. The Cu(OAc)<sub>2</sub>/PAN nanofibers were stabilized at 280 °C in air for 3 h and carbonized at 700 °C in 5 % H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere for 4 h to obtain Cu/C nanofibers. After that, the Cu/C nanofibers were soaked into the acetonitrile solution containing 15 mmol L<sup>−1</sup> TCNQ for 20 min and washed with anhydrous acetonitrile. CuTCNQ powder (CuTCNQ/PDs) was prepared as described previously. CuI acetonitrile solution (4.7 mmol L<sup>−1</sup>) and TCNQ acetonitrile solution (3 mmol L<sup>−1</sup>) were mixed rapidly. The mixed solution was stirred for 5 min. The whole process operated under an inert atmosphere at room temperature. The final dark blue product of CuTCNQ/PDs was collected by filtration and washed with a large amount of acetonitrile.</p>
            
            <p><b>Material characterization</b>: Materials were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, FEI SIRION 200, Holland) coupled with an energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectrometer, transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Tecnai G2 F30, Holland), powder X-ray diffraction (XRD, PANalytical B.V., Holland), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, VG MultiLab 2000 system with a monochromatic AlK<sub>α</sub> X-ray source).</p>
            
            <p><b>Electrochemical measurements</b>: CuTCNQ/CNFs films were cut into disks and directly used as working electrodes in CR2032 coin-type cells, whereas sodium metal pellets were applied as counter electrodes. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with a glass fiber membrane as the separator, 1.0 mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbon (EC)/propylene carbonate (PC) (v/v=1:1) as the electrolyte and a thin carbon film as interlayer. For CuTCNQ/PDs, the working electrodes were fabricated by coating the slurry containing 50 wt % CuTCNQ, 40 wt % super P carbon, and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder onto a carbon-coated aluminum foil. Other components were the same as the CuTCNQ/CNFs cells. Galvanostatic charge/discharge was performed on a multichannel battery testing system (Neware, China) and CVs were measured by CHI600D (Shanghai, China) in the voltage range of 2.0–4.1 V vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> at room temperature. EIS was performed on a PARSTAT 2273 potentiostat over the frequency ranging from 100 kHz to 0.1 Hz.</p>
            </section>
         
         <div class="article-section__content">
